0307474259|4.0|Gary Taubes' book Good Calories, Bad Calories (or 'The Diet Delusion' in Australia) is one of the most important health books I have ever read.<br /><br />The author is incredibly intelligent and that this book took the author more than five years to write, shows. I've read few health books so intelligently written as this one. I thought I was quite well educated about diet and the need to restrict refined carbohydrates (for good health and to stop weight gain) but I learned so much from reading this book.<br /><br />This book gives you a detailed analysis of why low calorie diets don't work and why restricted carbohydrate/high fat diets do and is backed up by compelling evidence and research at every point. We have evolved to eat a diet that contains enough fat and protein to cause satiety, lots of green vegetables and minimal amounts of fruits and starchy vegetables. Our bodies really can't cope with huge levels of refined carbohydrate as have recently been added to the modern diet.<br /><br />Because this book was so good but so very long and complex, I really hoped that Taubes would put out a summarised edition of the book that I could lend to my friends and family. I was so happy to see 'Why We Get Fat' had been released and bought a copy right away.<br /><br />While this 'distilled' book explains the same concepts and comes to the same conclusions as Taubes' previous book, and also explains some concepts in brilliant and remarkable new ways, I am not sure I would have been quite as blown away as I was by Taubes' work if I had read this book first. I couldn't help but think it was somewhat less compelling and made the various points just a little bit less convincingly. Perhaps that is inevitable with a much shorter book and comparing them is unfair? That might well be true.<br /><br />What I would have liked is for each of the main points to be listed one after the other in one chapter and explained using about a half page for each. To make it really simple for everyone to get the main points.<br /><br />Main points would include facts such as:<br /><br />1. The 'calories in, calories out' mantra is a myth<br /><br />2. 'A calorie is a calorie is a calorie' is a myth<br /><br />3. The 'just eat less and do more exercise to lose weight' message seems to be logical but is actually wrong and unhelpful<br /><br />4. Overweight and obese people often eat no more calories, or even less, than their thinner counterparts<br /><br />5. Low calorie diets also reduce the amount of nutrients in the diet<br /><br />6. It is a myth that the brain and CNS needs 120 - 130 grams of carbohydrate as fuel in order to function properly, as the body can use fat and protein equally as well, and these fuels are likely the mixture our brains have evolved to prefer.<br /><br />7. Restricting calories with a low fat/high carb diet just makes you hungrier and more lethargic and slows your metabolic rate. Weight loss is only maintained if the patients stays on a semi-starvation diet forever, which is impossible for most people and also undesirable. Being far more active just makes you far more hungry.<br /><br />8. It is a myth that reducing calories slightly or increasing activity slightly will lead to weight loss.<br /><br />9. It is a myth that we evolved through periods of feast and famine to be very good at holding onto fat. Fat gain is due to excessive insulin levels caused by high dietary refined carbohydrate intake. It is a sign of something in the body going wrong, not a healthy adaptation.<br /><br />10. Fructose is not much better than glucose and the two together may cause more harm than either individually.<br /><br />11. The idea of a weight 'set point' is a myth<br /><br />12. Insulin is the overall fuel control for mammals. High insulin levels cause the body to store fat and stop the body from using fat as fuel. This means that high carbohydrate foods make you put on more fat, and also leave you still feeling very hungry and unsatisfied.<br /><br />13. Our bodies have evolved to do best on a diet of plentiful fat and protein (including saturated fat), lots of greens and minimal fruits and starchy vegetables. This diet is the best for health and also for losing weight and stopping weight gain.<br /><br />14. Dietary fat, including saturated fat, is not a cause of obesity. Refined and easily digestible carbs causing high insulin levels cause obesity.<br /><br />15. To say that people are overweight due to gluttony and slothfulness is just not correct and it is very unfair. Overeating and a sedentary lifestyle are often CAUSED by eating a high carbohydrate diet! This association has wrongly been interpreted as a cause of weight gain, rather than an effect.<br /><br />16. Hunger caused by eating a high carbohydrate diet (or excessive exercising while on a low calorie diet) is a very strong physiological drive and should not be thought of something mild and psychological that can be overcome with willpower. This is something serious occurring in the body, not the brain!<br /><br />Thus psychological 'treatments' for obesity are inappropriate and cruel. Most people are overweight due to bad medical advice, NOT a lack of willpower, greed, laziness or because they lack 'moral fibre'<br /><br />17. People have different insulin secretory responses. Even if insulin secretion is slightly off, weight gain can occur.<br /><br />18. Eating large amounts of a high sugar and high fat food like popcorn is easy because the body will not use most of the carbohydrate and fat for immediate fuel but will store much of it as fat - leaving you able to eat a lot of it and still be hungry a short time later as well.<br /><br />19. Eating foods with a large bulk or high in fibre wont fill you up, you need the correct proportion of macronutrients and will stay hungry until you get them.<br /><br />20. Those advocating the low calorie and high carb diets for health and weight loss are not involved in legitimate science. These approaches are not supported by the evidence.<br /><br />I took 6 pages of notes while reading this book. Even though it is short, it does still give you a ton of information and research. It isn't one of those books stuffed with 'filler.'<br /><br />Reading the first book I wished the author had included some sort of basic eating plan that followed his principles. After reading this book, I wish the author had not included the basic eating plan he gives at the end of the  book. I realise that it is probably only there for illustrative purposes, but it really is of quite poor quality. Yes, it describes a low-carb diet which will be helpful for weight loss...but it is very far from being a healthy diet with regard to additives, nutrients and so on. This is an important failure when one of the main reasons many of us wish to lose weight is to improve our health.<br /><br />Eggs are not even mentioned in the eating plan which is quite bizarre. Even worse, microwaving is recommended and processed meats are allowed to be eaten. (Pork rinds, pepperoni, etc.) The diet allows up to 4 tablespoons of mayo daily, despite the fact this often contains unhealthy types of fat including trans fats. Aspartame, splenda and saccharin are recommended too, I was surprised to see. No mention is made of the importance of choosing grass-fed meats over conventionally farmed meats, if possible, and coconut oil is also not even mentioned.<br /><br />The diet recommends 3 cups of vegetables daily which is okay, but for my preferences, I don't see why one can't eat quite a bit more than that if one chooses green leafy vegetables which are of course very low carb. Choosing what to eat is about weight control, but getting as many nutrients in is also important and this is especially true if you are ill and trying to become more well. I also just like eating lots of nice veggies with my meals, and for me 3 cups is just not enough long term. (Been there done that!)<br /><br />Far better books forgiving you practical diet information for weight control and health are [[ASIN:0452285666 Eat Fat, Lose Fat: The Healthy Alternative to Trans Fats]] and [[ASIN:1594774137 Primal Body, Primal Mind: Beyond the Paleo Diet for Total Health and a Longer Life]], among others.<br /><br />Even with the above small quibbles, this is still an impressive body of work. I wish we had more investigative journalists writing about 'controversial' topics to such a high standard. I'm grateful to Taubes for writing his two books.<br /><br />I highly recommend this book or (if you are up to reading a very, very big and dense book) [[ASIN:1400033462 Good Calories, Bad Calories: Fats, Carbs, and the Controversial Science of Diet and Health (Vintage)]] would be even better. Check your library for a copy of one of them, at least! This information is so important.<br /><br />I give the first book 5 stars and this summary book 4.5 stars.<br /><br />Jodi Bassett, The Hummingbirds' Foundation for M.E. (HFME) and Health, Healing & Hummingbirds (HHH)e approaches are not supported by the evidence.<br /><br />I took 6 pages of notes while reading this book. 0143113100|4.0|The book gives the message that all sorts of amazing things can be done through lots of brain retraining work, but doesn't claim that it is a quick and easy cure for everything either, which I appreciated.<br /><br />Some people are taking the idea of neuroplasticity and running with it and maybe going a bit too far and claiming that we can retrain our brains to be any way we want (such as choosing our sexuality), and to use it to cure completely every neurological disease. It is disturbing. But this book doesn't at all support these sorts of unreasonable notions, fortunately.<br /><br />This book explains that amazing changes can be made to improve people with congenital issues, diseases or brain injuries or that wish to reverse negative learned behaviours - but that these changes take lots of hard work and also that they are limited in some cases. Recovery from brain injuries will often be partial and not total. Not all neurological function that is lost through a brain injury can be fully restored. All we can do is try our hardest and do our best.<br /><br />(The idea that everyone so injured could be totally well again if they just put the time into rehabilitation worries me a little. Again, this idea is not supported in the book, I'm referring to worrying and ignorant comments made elsewhere.)<br /><br />Partial recoveries are still very very exciting though and nothing to sneeze at, especially when people are very ill and disabled to begin with. It is very exciting stuff, this book.<br /><br />This book would be very encouraging to read for those who have had strokes and would like to learn to walk again. The bits at the start of the book about how neuroplasticity may allow people that are blind or deaf from birth to see and to hear, were astounding to read about.<br /><br />I was fascinated to read about how one can compensate for a failed vestibular system with vision, as this is something I and many other M.E. patients have done.<br /><br />I couldn't help but think about this compensation in M.E. patients as I read the book. It is a natural phenomena, which is kind of fascinating to me. But it is only a partial solution - which sort of supports the notions in this book of targeted and purposeful brain retraining being so powerful I guess.<br /><br />This natural brain retraining of the vestibular system works well, but leaves you in a hideous situation if you are even plunged into a totally dark environment - such as when the power goes out. You suddenly have no sense of up or down and the world spins horribly, so much so that you are too ill to crawl towards the torch or lighter that can save you.<br /><br />I keep torches within reach in every room I spend time in, I have to. This problem also means many of us need to sleep with a nightlight, as your body seems to need to know up from down when you are sleeping too. I also need to make sure when I walk that nothing in the room moves, or else I feel about to fall over and lose my equilibrium for a few minutes and have to clutch at things to not fall over.<br /><br />But far better all these problems than going back to when I didn't really have any sort of vastibular system and would be constantly spinning and feeling I was rolling off my flat bed, or feel I was falling backwards the whole time I was walking upright! As the women in the book said too, it is also awful always walking with your feet so wide apart for balance as well. I'm so used to it now, but it would be wonderful to one day no longer have to do so! Maybe in time, and with the ideas in this book put into use, this could happen? I'd like to think so.<br /><br />Reading about how well-known these visual compensations for vestibular problems are was a bit hard going for me at times, as it made me quite annoyed and sad that so few doctors and neurologists seem to know about them and that we as patients are each treated as if this problem were unique to us, when it is so obviously not. The woman with vestibular symptoms mentioned in this book described my symptoms so well. It is a bit maddening how behind with real cutting-edge research and information so many medical professionals are.<br /><br />If I can improve my health to the point of being out and about in the world again, and my vestibular function hasn't improved along with the rest of me, I'll definitely try the under-tongue vestibular re-trainer device (I forget the proper name for it used in the book!) if I can.<br /><br />This book was exciting to read and gave me hope for my future and one day being able to try out some of these ideas for myself. But for right now, with a brain injury that also has had systemic effects and caused cardiac insufficiency and an inability to do anything but the most basic tasks (I'm housebound and 95% bedbound and very limited with talking and movement etc. as well), it is impossible and would result in relapse and loss of function rather than improvements. Having an ongoing disease process which means your activity level is severely restricted as well as neurological problems is a different kettle of fish to fixing neurological issues which are not ongoing, such as a stroke, say. You need to be able to put the work in, and not have a disease which is worsening for physical reasons too I guess. But hope for the future is still hope.<br /><br />The parts supporting animal studies in the book made me feel quite uncomfortable. I would personally rather they had been omitted from the book as they detract from the important main message.<br /><br />I also have to admit that I was a bit disappointed after reading the book and hearing so many good things said about it that it wasn't edited to a somewhat higher standard, and organised a bit better. Parts of the book were quite boring to read and it'd have been great if the book had summarised the main points in the final chapter instead of just kind of petering out. The book was well written, but could maybe have been made even better with a good edit.<br /><br />All through the book I kept wondering how much more positive the results from these treatments would be if they were combined with patients being given all the nutrients their brains need to function well and to heal. Nutritional medicine is vital for anyone recovering form a brain injury or similar, the body needs to have all the basic building blocks necessary to make repairs and to function properly, no matter what other treatments are tried. Although this topic is mostly ignored in this book, there was one comment towards the end where the author said that although nutrition was not his area that a properly designed supplement regime is 'a common practice that we should all be pursuing' which is hard to argue with.<br /><br />For anyone interested in this topic books such as [[ASIN:1887202048 Detoxify or Die]], [[ASIN:0452285666 Eat Fat, Lose Fat: The Healthy Alternative to Trans Fats]], [[ASIN:0615228380 Deep Nutrition: Why Your Genes Need Traditional Food]] and [[ASIN:1594774137 Primal Body, Primal Mind: Beyond the Paleo Diet for Total Health and a Longer Life]] and others are a great start. (These books and others like them have let me very slowly start to improve my own health, FWIW.)<br /><br />Overall this book is a fascinating and engaging read, especially if you are facing a neurological disease or other neurological problem. I'd highly recommend checking to see if your library has a copy. The books on neurological case studies by Oliver Sacks are also a very interesting read if you have any type of brain injury.<br /><br />Jodi Bassett, The Hummingbirds' Foundation for M.E. (HFME) and Health, Healing & Hummingbirds (HHH)bular symptoms mentioned in this book described my symptoms so well. It is a bit maddening how behind with real cutting-edge research and information so many medical professionals are.<br /><br />If I can improve my health to the point of being out and about in the world again, and my vestibular function hasn't improved along with the rest of me, I'll definitely try the under-tongue vestibular re-trainer device (I forget the proper name for it used in the book!) if I can.<br /><br />This book was exciting to read and gave me hope for my future and one day being able to try out some of these ideas for myself. Although this topic is mostly ignored in this book, there was one comment towards the end where the author said that although nutrition was not his area that a properly designed supplement regime is 'a common practice that we should all be pursuing' which is hard to argue with.<br /><br />For anyone interested in this topic books such as [[ASIN:1887202048 Detoxify or Die]], [[ASIN:0452285666 Eat Fat, Lose Fat: The Healthy Alternative to Trans Fats]], [[ASIN:0615228380 Deep Nutrition: Why Your Genes Need Traditional Food]] and [[ASIN:1594774137 Primal Body, Primal Mind: Beyond the Paleo Diet for Total Health and a Longer Life]] and others are a great start. (These books and others like them have let me very slowly start to improve my own health, FWIW.)<br /><br />Overall this book is a fascinating and engaging read, especially if you are facing a neurological disease or other neurological problem. I'd highly recommend checking to see if your library has a copy. The books on neurological case studies by Oliver Sacks are also a very interesting read if you have any type of brain injury.<br /><br />Jodi Bassett, The Hummingbirds' Foundation for M.E. (HFME) and Health, Healing & Hummingbirds (HHH)

0060899220|5.0|Anthony Bourdain is honest and forthright about his personal and professional life in the restaurant business.  He covered a lot of areas, and this is also a book for those considering going into the business. KC begins with Bourdain realizing the tastiness and special qualities of superb food on a trip to France with his parents as a child.  Later, after working in a Summer for a mid-range restaurant in the Cape of Mass, Bourdain entered a 2-year culinary school.  After graduation he got a job at a well known restaurant atop the Rockefeller Center in New York. A lot of wealthy New York influential \\"eating garbage at the top of the world\\" as Bourdain put it, \\"with a view of Manhattan.\\"  He classifies cooks into 3 groups: artists, exiles, and mercenaries.  The artists are annoying and high maintenance, the exiles cannot make it in any other business because of idiosyncrasies and foibles.  The mercenaries do it for the money and do it well.  I think these categories can certainly be applied to employees in several other businesses and industries.  Author Bourdain noted the long hours, stress, heat, and conditions of working in close proximity to other people in a busy, hot kitchen.  The stress causes many to use drugs on the job to take the edge off.  He also notes the humor, camaraderie, vernacular, and slang of the crew in kitchens.  There is no political correctness, here.  Working with an array of sharp knives that are close at hand, leads to control of potential bullies and malcontents when personalities clash.  He notes the open and wild times of the 1970s in the business and outside of it.  Bourdain appreciates good, delicious cuisine.  As for the some of the many things that he notes in restaurants is that brunch is basically left-over food from things not used in the previous couple of days, and fish on Mondays is usually from Thursday.  If the fish not gotten rid of by Monday it's a waste and therefore a financial loss.  Hence Bourdain's advice: \\"don't order fish on Mondays.\\"  My only recommendation for KC is that it should have had a glossary in the back for restaurant terms and French words that were often used throughout the book.  Without a definition, the reader has to guess the meaning, and it was not always easy.  As for the bartenders that often accompany a restaurant, the most common way a bartender increases his income is the \\"buy-back.\\"  Every 2nd or 3rd drink/beer is on the bartender and the tip can be increased in appreciation.  Almost all of us have experience this.  I think it's more common in the big cities.   One saying: \\"An ounce of sauce can cover a multitude of sins,\\" if the meat is a little off.  Bourdain's mentor in the business when he became a manager and later an owner was a man with the nickname Bigfoot because of his physical scale.  Bourdain adopted Many of Bigfoot's principles.  One example, is having a restaurant supplier (called a 'purveyor') delivery driver unload an entire truck at the restaurant, only to re-load it and leave without Bourdain buying it because the driver was 5-minutes late.  A little picky here?    'Failing Restaurant Syndrome' is also detailed:  When a restaurant if failing, people often start to brainstorm and tinker with the menu, and make unnecessary and futile changes.  Different gimmicks are introduced such as \\"New Orleans Brunch complete with a Dixieland band,\\" and/or \\"Sunday night Brunch and prix fixe menus.\\"  A publicist is hired.  Each brainstorm is more and more counterproductive than the one before.  Last minute attempts to thwart the coming insolvency. Then the consultants are hired.  Bourdain notes that the consultants are the last ones to come in before paychecks bounce, vendors put the restaurant on COD and the repo men arrive.  A tough business, it is.    \\"Kitchen Confidential\\" is reader friendly and contains lots of personal info and details about the restaurant business.  Lots of pleasure about the restaurant industry for the customers (which we allare), those who enjoy good food, and for those in the industry and for those who are considering going into it.

0062082353|2.0|THE GOOD:  This book is a fairly fast read. For better or for worse though, it is pretty much written on a third-grade level. Now I'm not expecting a soldier, a SEAL no less to be a master thespian, but given the assistance Mr. Kyle had in writing this book, you would expect a little more depth.  A really nice touch to this book was the interwoven perspectives of his spouse, Taya. As one knows, every story has more than one side to it, and Mrs. Kyle adds an interesting layer to her spouse's story. It would have been refreshing, however, to get her take on what all those kills meant to her personally, i.e. how did she come to terms with that. But then again, she knew what she was getting into when she married him and married into the life.  THE BAD:  No real surprises here. Before I even turned a single page [a book I picked up to pass the time on a flight], this story has been told before. What is there really to be said that hasn't been said before?  THE UGLY:  Here is where the tough love comes in: Let's stop being silly, cut the bullcrap and be honest here. I'm confident in saying a majority of the reviewers here who gave it 5 stars have either one of three things pertaining to them. 1. They know Chris Kyle personally. 2. They are gung-ho ex-military types. or 3. They have a extreme right-wing view on life.  That said, let's see it for what it is: Chris Kyle is NOT a 'hero'. Period. The 'sacrifices' he supposedly made were actually selfish choices. This man LOVES to kill for the sake of killing, plain and simple. The 'honor' rationale he sprinkles as an afterthought throughout the book is cringing at times. Perhaps he has rationalized it in his psyche, and no doubt he created life-long bonds with his comrades in the heat of battle. But at the end of the day, he couldn't wait to go in the front lines and keep killing. You very quickly reach the conclusion that he enjoys it.  Another reoccurring theme where I found myself asking the same question over and over: Howis it that he can call the insurgents \\"savages\\", feeling so indifferent to killing them, picking them off like they were a video game, yet we are supposed to go along for his \\"payback\\", and feel empathy for him personally?  No love for them either, Mr. Kyle, but really now, would you be ANY different if you were a dog backed into a corner by a foreign army? I am sure some reviewers here will view this as being liberal or sympathetic in nature, but quite the contrary. Part of defeating your adversary is getting into the mind of your adversary. I can see Mr. Kyle and ourselves, for that matter, resorting to such tactics if we viewed foreigners as invading our soil.  Even if Mr. Kyle never enlisted to do something he relished doing anyhow, I assure you the world would have still kept on spinning. We all have our delusions at one time or another of not being expendable, so let's squash this, okay? And one other thing, there are more ways than what you did to serve your country and fulfill your domestic obligations. Happens every day.  Bottom line: Is Chris Kyle an elite warrior? No question about it. Is he a brave soldier? Undoubtedly. Is he a hero [a term used so loosely in today's society, that really now, people should pull out a dictionary]? A resounding 'NO'.  The unique thing about this author [aside from his kill number, which, pardon me Sir, were it not for that statistic you wouldn't even have a book published!]is that he was able to place his unique/special talent to use, a talent he LOVED possessing, into an arena whereby any other place he would be thrown into prison.  Amazing the depths we will go to in order to rationalize the things that we do.

Whatever your issue is, the author helps you understand why your brain wants to overeat, overshop, cheat on your spouse and gives helpful exercises for you to stop the behavior.   I read this book in a couple of sittings, but I'm going back through the way she recommends: a chapter a week to fully work through the exercise(s) I chose. I can't say this book has been life-changing for me; I've only had it a week, but I'm optimistic. The author teaches a class on willpower at Stanford, and she mentions that some people take it multiple times, and people bring their kids and spouses and co-workers in to it. I can believe it. This book is well-written and fun to read.   It's an empowering book-and since it's based on facts and science instead of pithy sayings and unusual if inspiring anecdotes-is more likely to get you to improve your life-with actual lasting results- than most self-help books out there. Seriously, stop reading the books that just tell you to wish upon a star and only reference motivation speakers to back up their claims. B000001F7R|4.0|I first heard Def Leppard (and it was probably the first of maybe 3 times total) on my local rock station shortly after this album came out - they played 'Rock Brigade' and it was like a breath of fresh air twisting its way through the stale vapors of what was being played on radio at that time...a few months later the band (along with Scorpions) opened up for Ted Nugent on his 'Scream Dream' tour (the day before my birthday...what a cool present!) - after that, I HAD to find the album.  Finally found it and became a huge fan.  Up until this period, I was more of a (oh, how I hate this term) 'classic' rock fan (Rush, Zep, Kiss, Foghat, Nazereth, UFO, etc etc etc), but the HEAVY stuff (Aerosmith, Triumph...you get the idea...) - this album, and this BAND basically kicked down the door for the New Wave Of British Heavy Metal (although there were some bands recording this type of stuff before the Lep's released this...Riot, Saxon, et al) and I was happily swept away on a wave of exciting new bands from both the UK as well as other countries.  Listening back on it now you can hear the naivety on a lot of these tracks, but I think that's what makes it so fun to listen to today.  I guess you needed to hear it when it was first released to fully appreciate what this album (as well as 'Hi 'N Dry') meant to metal fans back in the early '80s.  And it's strange...before they recorded this, the band were revered as metal heroes in their native Britain...for some odd reason, the album (and the band, after recording it) received extremely hostile reactions by both critics as well as fans in jolly olde England, essentially directing the band to focus their attention on the American market, hence the ultimate demise of the band...once Pete Willis left the group, Phil Collen (previously in a band called 'Girl' - a dead givaway of what was to come...) stepped in and WHAM!!! Inane, teeny-bopper fluff metal at it's 'finest.'  Gone were the thundering power chords that kicked down yourdoor to be replaced with chimey, polite chords that snuck around your door, begging to be let in...and those swelling harmony vocals in the chorus that just swept your heart away...BAH! And yes, I know Willis had an alcohol problem, but he was the 'bringer of the grit' and the band was never the same again after his departure...all the other 'positive' reviewers have already described what this album sounds like; I just felt like adding my 2 cents to it as well...I can still hear the majesty and power of 'It Could Be You,' still my favorite Def Lep song ever...

0812992792|5.0|If you're thinking about buying this book, you should know this: this novel will take you places you have never seen.  And those images will stay with you.  Perhaps the most remarkable aspect of this book is Adam Johnson's ability to combine research (that is, he is one of the few Americans to visit North Korea) with an intuitive understanding of the human soul.  This is not merely a political or a geographic novel--though of course it is those things as well--it is a masterfully plotted story that lures its characters into a place where they must know themselves--their loves and ambitions--in a way that is devastating.  In other words, this is not only the best literary I've read in the past year; it is also the first novel to realistically explore the oppressive regime of North Korea.  If you've read Johnson's previous books, you've no doubt noticed his attraction to dark humor and political absurdities, a stance that is always coupled with a deep understanding of the human condition.  Johnson's short stories--somewhat like those of George Saunders--often create an absurdist alternate American reality, one pitched just right to offer cultural commentary on our current social climate.  In terms of this book's design, its genius is that Johnson has found the perfect location for his absurdist political outlook, a place where propaganda disguises itself as news, a place where oppression is presented as love, a country populated by millions yet with only one identifiable individual citizen, its ruler, Kim Jong Il.  This is an amazing read.  You'll be glad you picked it up.

B006KAWI2C|4.0|NOTE: This review was revised upon my discovery that my box was missing disc 11, had two copies of disc 13, and was lacking disc 94 (and jacket).<br /><br />The recordings of Arthur Rubinstein have been well treated on Compact Disc.  Some of RCA's first CD issues of analogue recordings were devoted to Rubinstein, although the early remastering left much to be desired.  (I worked at a classical record store on Cleveland's Shaker Square when a Tchaikovsky/Grieg disc was issued.  The manager said it would never sell because it originated as an analogue recording - in fact all our copies sold within two days.) Most of Rubinstein's stereo recordings have received multiple CD issues, a testament to the pianist's continuing popularity with the public.  The mono recordings have not had such broad distribution which has contributed to a distorted picture of the pianist's legacy.  RCA's Complete Rubinstein Collection, issued as an expensive box in 1999, gathered together all of Rubinstein's recordings for RCA, HMV/EMI, and a single recording he made for Decca/London - it also contained a two CD set of interviews and previously unissued recordings.  Now we have this Original Album Collection.  Contained herein is everything from the 1999 box, except for the 1964 Moscow recital, which is available elsewhere on CD and DVD.  This set also includes three CDs of previously unpublished live recordings (more on those later)  -  making for a total of over 100 hours containing a huge swath of solo, concerto, and chamber repertoire -  at a fraction of the price of the 1999 box.<br /><br />With the exception of a 78RPM disc made for the Polish Favorit label in 1910 (never transferred to a modern format), Rubinstein refused to record during the pre-electric era - he complained the old acoustic recording process made the piano sound \\"like a banjo\\".  So the earliest recording here was made when Rubinstein was already 41 years old.  It's fascinating to trace Rubinstein's evolution as an interpreter over a 48 year period.<br /><br />In general, Rubinstein's earlier performances of shorter works, such as Chopin Mazurkas and Nocturnes, are freer and more imaginative than the later remakes.  The younger Rubinstein also has greater control of pianissimo, which can clearly be heard even in the earliest recordings.  Conversely, Rubinstein's sense of structure, particularly in larger scale works, improved with advancing years.  The pianist's stereo remakes of Beethoven's Opp. 13, 31, No. 3, and 81a sonatas, Chopin's Op. 35 sonata, and the Brahms Op. 5 sonata, are head and shoulders above the earlier versions.  The flip side is that, by the 1960s, Rubinstein had become conscious of his role as an \\"elder-statesman\\" of the piano and that he was recording \\"for posterity\\" - resulting in a loss of spontaneity.  Also, his recordings from the mid-1960s onward are increasingly marred by the lack of a true pianissimo in his playing - possibly a result of deteriorating hearing.  Many of the pianist's mid-period recordings, such as the Chopin Polonaises from 1951, marry the best of both worlds.<br /><br />We are doubly blessed that many of Rubinstein's best performed recordings (especially in concertos) were also made during RCA's \\"Living Stereo\\" era - when that company's recording engineers were putting out their best work.  By the mid-1960s, RCA's engineers were experimenting with multiple-miking which marred his later concerto recordings, despite digital remastering<br /><br />Now to the newly issued material: These stem from the 1961 series of ten Carnegie Hall recitals given by Rubinstein (truly \\"given\\", as he donated the entire proceeds to charity).  Four works are new to Rubinstein's recorded repertoire: Falla's Dance of the Miller's Wife; Debussy's L'Isle Joyeuse; Scriabin's Nocturne for the Left Hand, and Stravinsky's Petrouchka.  The Scriabin is proof that Rubinstein's left-hand technique was secure, despite an inconsequential wrong note at the beginning. The Falla and Debussy have an orgiastic brio that allows one to forgive the many missing details.  Performances like these must have led Harold Schonberg to write that \\"Rubinstein played with the great pelvic thrust\\". The Stravinsky is most interesting, as it was written expressly for Rubinstein (with the pianist's assistance) and the composer gave Rubinstein carte-blanche to make any changes he felt appropriate.  The most significant change is that Rubinstein reinstates the \\"drum-roll\\" interludes between the three scenes.  The Russian Dance starts out roughly, with several wrong notes and some unsteadiness, before settling down for the scene in Petrouchka's Room. In the Shrovetide Fair scene, Rubinstein throws caution to the wind and gives a full throttle, adrenaline fused performance - overwhelming in its savagery.  This is the Rubinstein that music lovers over a certain age reminisce about - one never heard in the more cautious studio recordings.  The remaining works have been recorded elsewhere by Rubinstein, but when performing live, the pianist was notably more flexible, poetic, and exciting than in the studio.<br /><br />The set also includes two DVDs: Rubinstein Remembered, a 1987 documentary hosted by the pianist's son, John Rubinstein; and Recital for Israel - a 1975 performance at Ambassador College in Pasadena, California.  There is also a CD only version of this recital, although the playing is far from Rubinstein's best and I have no doubt the pianist would be displeased by its release.<br /><br />This set uses the best existing transfers of each recording, which in most cases means the superb 1999 remasters by Jon Samuels.  A few exceptions include SACD hybrid remasters of selected Living Stereo items.  Of course, the three discs of new material are newly remastered, but the source material (monaural cassette tapes owned by John Rubinstein, and RCA tapes recorded using an AMPEX noise reduction curve that flattened Rubinstein's tone), is less than optimal.  Still, these remain eminently listenable.  The sound in the Ambassador College DVD has been sweetened and is more palatable than previous issues - though still far from ideal.<br /><br />The quality control issues prevent me from giving this a five star review.one to forgive the many missing details.  Performances like these must have led Harold Schonberg to write that \\"Rubinstein played with the great pelvic thrust\\". The Stravinsky is most interesting, as it was written expressly for Rubinstein (with the pianist's assistance) and the composer gave Rubinstein carte-blanche to make any changes he felt appropriate.  The most significant change is that Rubinstein reinstates the \\"drum-roll\\" interludes between the three scenes.  The Russian Dance starts out roughly, with several wrong notes and some unsteadiness, before settling down for the scene in Petrouchka's Room. In the Shrovetide Fair scene, Rubinstein throws caution to the wind and gives a full throttle, adrenaline fused performance - overwhelming in its savagery. Of course, the three discs of new material are newly remastered, but the source material (monaural cassette tapes owned by John Rubinstein, and RCA tapes recorded using an AMPEX noise reduction curve that flattened Rubinstein's tone), is less than optimal. The check list goes on.  I know this review is a collection of checklists; but this is exactly how I felt reading the book. A set of checklists to create an irresistible thriller. 0060833459|5.0|Note: Amazon continues to feature reviews of earlier editions. What immediately follows is my review of the 50th anniversary edition published today, January 24, 2017.  What then follows is my review of an earlier edition.<br />*  *  *<br /><br />This is the 50th anniversary edition of a book first published in 1967. Jim Collins provides the Foreword and Zachary First the Afterword. In my opinion, Peter Drucker (1909-2005) is the most influential business thinker as indicated by the endless list of other thought leaders who continue to acknowledge his value and significance to their own work. He always insisted on referring to himself as a student or bystander. With all due respect to his wishes, I have always viewed him as a pioneer who surveyed and defined dimensions of the business world that no one else had previously explored.<br /><br />Consider this passage in the Foreword: Here are ten lessons I learned from Peter Drucker and this book, and that I offer as a small portal of entry into the mind of the greatest management thinker off all time. These are the lessons that Collins cites and discusses:<br /><br />1. First, manage thyself.<br />2. Do what youre made for.<br />3. Work how you work best (and let others do the same).<br />4. Count your time, and make it count.<br />5. Prepare better meetings.<br />6. Dont make a hundred decisions when one will do.<br />7. Find your one big distinctive impact.<br />8. Stop what you would not start.<br />9. Run lean.<br />10. Be useful.<br /><br />He was in the end, Collins adds, &#34;the highest level of what a teacher can be: a role model of the very ideas he taught, a walking testament to his teachings in the tremendous lasting effect of his own life.<br /><br />As was true of Collins and will be true 0f everyone else who reads one of the several editions, they will have their own take-aways. Drucker provides a framework in the Introduction, stressing while discussing the importance of eight specific practices that all great business and non-profit CEOs are committed to, such as asking What needs to be done? and What is right for the enterprise? The first two enable them to obtain the information they need.<br /><br />The next four help them to convert this knowledge into effective action:<br /><br />3. Develop action plans.<br />4. Take responsibility for decisions [and their consequences].<br />5. Take responsibility for communicating.<br />6. Are focused on opportunities rather on problems.<br /><br />The last two ensure that the entire organization feels responsible and accountable<br /><br />7. Run productive meetings.<br />8. Think and feel we rather than I.<br /><br />Yes, these are basic and obvious practices but they were not five decades ago. Until Drucker, thinking about management lacked order, structure, clarity, and focus. Borrowing a phrase from Oliver Wendell Holmes, Drucker developed thinking about management to the other side of complexity. To paraphrase, Albert Einstein, Drucker made management as simple as possible but no simpler.<br /><br />In the Introduction Peter Drucker concludes, Weve just covered eight practices of effective executives. Im going to throw in one final, bonus practice. This ones so important that Ill elevate it to the level of a rule: [begin italics] Listen first, speak last [end italics]...And, like every discipline, effectiveness [begin italics] can [end italics] and [begin italics] must [end italics] be earned.<br /><br />The title of this review is a portion of one of Peter Drucker's most important insights: &#34;The most serious mistakes are not being made as a result of wrong answers. The true dangerous thing is asking the wrong question.&#34;<br /><br />*  *  *<br /><br />I first read this book when it was originally published in 1967 and have since re-read it several times because, in my opinion, it provides some of Peter Drucker's most important insights on how to &#34;get the right work done and done the right way.&#34; By nature an &#34;executive&#34; is one who &#34;executes,&#34; producing a desired result (an &#34;effect&#34;) that has both impact and value. As Drucker once observed in an article that appeared in Harvard Business Review at least 40 years ago, &#34;There is surely nothing quite so useless as doing with great efficiency what should not be done at all.&#34; Therefore, the effective executive must develop sound judgment. Difficult - sometimes immensely difficult - decisions must be made. Here are eight practices that Drucker recommended 45 years ago:<br /><br />o  Ask, &#34;what needs to be done?&#34;<br />o  Ask, &#34;What is right for the enterprise?&#34;<br />o  Develop an action plan<br />o  Take responsibility for decisions.<br />o  Take responsibility for communications.<br />o  Focus on opportunities rather than on problems.<br />o  Conduct productive meetings.<br />o Think in terms of first-person PLURAL pronouns (&#34;We&#34; rather than &#34;I&#34;).<br /><br />The first two practices give executives the knowledge they need; the next four help them convert this knowledge into effective action; the last two ensure that the entire organization feels responsible and accountable, and will thus be more willing to become engaged. &#34;I'm going to throw in one final, bonus practice. This one's so important that I'll elevate it to the level of a rule: [begin italics] Listen first, speak last.&#34; [end italics]<br /><br />This volume consists of eight separate but interdependent essays that begin with &#34;Effectiveness Can Be Learned&#34; and conclude with &#34;Effective Decisions.&#34; Actually, there is a &#34;Conclusion&#34; in which Drucker asserts that &#34;Effectiveness Must Be Learned.&#34; I agree. The essays are arranged in a sequence that parallels a learning process that prepares an executive to &#34;assume responsibility, rather than to act the subordinate, satisfied only if he `pleases the boss.' In focusing himself and his vision on contribution the executive, in other words, has to think through purposes and ends rather than means alone.&#34;<br /><br />I highly recommend this to all executives who need an easy-to-read collection of reminders of several basic but essential insights from one of the most important business thinkers, Peter Drucker. I also presume to suggest that they, in turn, urge each of their direct reports to obtain a copy and read it. The last time I checked, Amazon sells a paperbound edition for only $11.55. Its potential value is incalculable.ne the right way.&#34; By nature an &#34;executive&#34; is one who &#34;executes,&#34; producing a desired result (an &#34;effect&#34;) that has both impact and value. As Drucker once observed in an article that appeared in Harvard Business Review at least 40 years ago, &#34;There is surely nothing quite so useless as doing with great efficiency what should not be done at all.&#34; Therefore, the effective executive must develop sound judgment. Difficult - sometimes immensely difficult - decisions must be made. Here are eight practices that Drucker recommended 45 years ago:<br /><br />o  Ask, &#34;what needs to be done?&#34;<br />o  Ask, &#34;What is right for the enterprise?&#34;<br />o  Develop an action plan<br />o  Take responsibility for decisions.<br />o  Take responsibility for communications.<br />o  Focus on opportunities rather than on problems.<br />o  Conduct productive meetings.<br />o Think in terms of first-person PLURAL pronouns (&#34;We&#34; rather than &#34;I&#34;).<br /><br />The first two practices give executives the knowledge they need; the next four help them convert this knowledge into effective action; the last two ensure that the entire organization feels responsible and accountable, and will thus be more willing to become engaged. &#34;I'm going to throw in one final, bonus practice. This one's so important that I'll elevate it to the level of a rule: [begin italics] Listen first, speak last.&#34; [end italics]<br /><br />This volume consists of eight separate but interdependent essays that begin with &#34;Effectiveness Can Be Learned&#34; and conclude with &#34;Effective Decisions.&#34; Actually, there is a &#34;Conclusion&#34; in which Drucker asserts that &#34;Effectiveness Must Be Learned.&#34; I agree. The essays are arranged in a sequence that parallels a learning process that prepares an executive to &#34;assume responsibility, rather than to act the subordinate, satisfied only if he `pleases the boss.' In focusing himself and his vision on contribution the executive, in other words, has to think through purposes and ends rather than means alone.&#34;<br /><br />I highly recommend this to all executives who need an easy-to-read collection of reminders of several basic but essential insights from one of the most important business thinkers, Peter Drucker. Its potential value is incalculable.

This is so critical, it is one of the first tasks a child starts learning.<br /><br />This app is great in theory, as it helps a young child (2-5) with developing these skills. Any practice a child gets in this critical period should be welcomed with applause. Additionally, a child that young isn't typically using technology alone, which is the second major advantage. Anytime a parent gets involved in her/his child's learning, there are numerous benefits to both involved.<br /><br />There were only two major problems I found with the game. First was the developer's significant lack of knowledge regarding child development. Generally children this age can discriminate in such categories as color, food groups, letters/numbers, etc. They DON'T know (usually) the animal classifications, nor would they typically know the difference between a planet and a satellite.<br /><br />Second, there should be an ascending order of difficulty to the items. There doesn't seem to be any rationale for the different levels, except that a level may only have four questions. (Great understanding of a preschooler's attention span!) B0014Z4OQG|3.0|For most people, you either loved this film, or you completely despised it. As for me, there were things that I loved about this film, and things that I didn't care for. Cloverfield follows several twenty-somethings as they try to escape a city being attacked by a giant Godzilla-like creature.<br />I think that this film was a landmark in motion picture history in two ways. The first landmark is how they campaigned for the film. It was brilliant. Successfully keeping this movie in the dark, they released a trailer that showed us very little and only had a date instead of a title. People who looked up this date online found a website that only had a couple pictures. From here, the advertisers used a number of techniques to publicize the film and pique audiences' interest. The main website would be updated occasionally with pictures that gave clues about the stories. Several of the characters had MySpace pages and online video blogs. There were several fake news and business websites created to help give further background to the story. All in all, it was an amazing form of advertising that got audiences excited for the film and accounted for a great deal of profit that the studio could not have made any other way.<br />The second landmark is the way they filmed the movie. This is where most audience members grew to hate the film. The movie was filmed as if we saw everything from a handheld camera being used by one of the characters. This added a great deal of realism and believability to the story where we felt like we were watching a YouTube-like video of an actual event. But this also meant that there was a lot of camera-shake in the process and anyone who had motion-sickness did not make it far into the movie. While it's true that [[ASIN:B00001QGUM The Blair Witch Project]] started the hand-held technique, I believe it was this film that showed the true potential and effectiveness of filming in this form. It was this film that started the hand-held craze and later inspired other films like [[ASIN:B001MVYUR0 Quarantine]] and [[ASIN:B002VKE1K2 Paranormal Activity]].<br />Aside from that, the movie itself wasn't all that good. The movie echoed Godzilla a lot, so we felt like we had seen this film a dozen times before. There was really nothing to new to see. Another thing that bothered me was the lack of background story in the film. A lot of the background information was covered in the campaign sites for the film, but if you never looked at any of those, you were left with a lot of questions.<br /><br />**Spoiler Alert**<br />Apparently, - and they don't tell you this in the film anywhere - the object landing in the ocean at the end of the film was a failed satellite that crashed to the earth and awoke a monster that had been hibernating underwater for thousands of years. 1401232590|2.0|The real tragedy of this title is not the weak, contrived ending nor the bizarre and pointless parade of Batman's Rogue Gallery . . . it's that Sale and Loeb could have really put together a fantastic Batman mystery if they'd been pressed to.  Let me start off that Sale's artwork tends to be a little polarizing. People love it or they hate it, and it is very stylized. I enjoyed the moody, noir feeling Sale's deep shadows and muted colors evoke, I enjoyed the architecture of Gotham, and there were many splash pages that were artistically satisfying. Some weak anatomy and draftsmanship mistakes conveniently blacked out by a dramatic shadow are hardly unique to Tim Sale, and credit must be given where it's due. The Long Halloween has some very well-drawn pages and panels.  Loeb, on the other hand, really wastes the opportunity to write a beefy, 300+ page Batman mystery - something many writers would eat their own word processors to have the opportunity to do - by slathering his narrative with tired Godfather references, cliche Mafia tropes, and a hackneyed, clumsy mystery resolution that comes out of nowhere and makes no sense in the context of the story. I get the sense that Loeb was really into writing his mystery, but kept running into plot holes and lulls in the story that he had no idea how to work through . . . and that he wrote the ending before he wrote the story. His attempt to dove-tail his 'surprise' killer's motivations and drives with the slow and meandering narrative of his mystery is almost embarrassing to wade through. It's like having to watch a stand-up comedian bomb on stage . . . for almost 400 pages.  Sadly, the build-up to the reveal is actually interesting, the interest in the killer's identity grows as the story unfolds. The Harvey Dent sub-plot, while predictable and very cookie-cutter Batman story-telling, is nevertheless solid and holds up as the tale progresses. But the ending, the mystery resolution and the steps that lead to it are so badthat they overshadow and eclipse the aspects of the book that succeed at what they try to do.  The Long Halloween is a decent enough read if you don't want to think too critically about the story and/or attempt to solve the mystery before the characters do. Sale's artwork is well worth seeing, although it's quality is a topic of subjectivity. But if you're in the mood for a meatier, more cerebral treatment of Gotham's Dark Knight . . . you won't find it here.

0385333846|3.0|Slaughterhouse Five is, in effect, an autobiography of Kurt Vonnegut's time during the World War Two. However, Vonnegut tries to put his own satirical spin on his life by creating the awkward, little soldier, Billy Pilgrim. Here's the real twist: Billy is unstuck in time. That is, he is able to perceive every moment of his life from any point in time. As if that's not confusing enough, Billy has no control over his visions. This creates a story that goes back and forth through flashbacks and flashforewards, creating a story so knotted up within itself that even the reader will lose all sense of what time it's supposed to be in the book.   The book seems to be intended to bring to light the Fire-Bombing of Dresden, an event many Americans would rather leave forgotten. However, the Fire-bombing, while hinted at throughout the book, only occurs near the very end, and even then, it seems that Billy's own emotions and stresses resulting from excessive time travel take the main focus of the book.   If you're into dark humor, confusing and intricate storylines, and rather depressing events, then this is the book for you! If, however, you'd rather turn your frown upside down, I suggest reading something else. For the tragic, yet grimly humorous and ironic tale of Billy Pilgrim and the Fire-bombing of Dresden doesn't exactly make for a giddy and gleeful read.

B00000J0O2|1.0|Cartridge supplied was *very* old - well past it's shelf life but I only found out after it dumped a load of toner into my previously pristine and perfectly working printer.  Not entirely sure of the actual cause of this but most likely due to the toner appearing caked to the ends of the roller and perhaps messing up the way that the toner is normally released.    After reading one reviewer's comment that the cartridge they had received was over 9 years old I researched how to find the date of manufacture for HP cartridges online.  What I found was there is a 6 character code beginning with a number followed by a letter.  The important bit is the first digit which is the last digit of the year.  In my case the code was 3B... meaning the cartridge was made in February 2003!   That's almost 9 years old!    A search online (including HP) suggests a shelf life of 2.5 years!  I don't think vendors should be selling cartridges that are past their shelf life.  So, in trying to save some money, and purchasing a cartridge that was not advertised as \\"9 year old cartridge that we probably bought for fractions of a penny on the dollar and selling to you for what appears to be just a reasonable price\\" I've ended up probably ruining a perfectly good printer.   In conclusion, there *may* be sellers who are genuinely offering a good deal on genuine HP cartridges, but my advise is to check the date code before installing and consider carefully if you want to proceed or request a refund if you think you have been sold \\"New but past the recommended shelf life\\" instead!

1597801585|4.0|This book follows half a dozen expatriates, American and others, in a future Thailand, after the world has shuddered through a mini-Dark Ages and has just emerged out the other side.  There are serious environmental challenges in this future world, and the geneticists of that world are fighting a 5-alarm battle to stay one step ahead of exotic diseases and conditions that threaten the world food supply.  The characters in the story are part of this struggle in various ways as it plays out across social, political, physical, religious and economic battlefields.  The plot is gripping and fast-paced, sometimes so much so that it broke me out of the story:  it's just not possible that this much crap can happen to so few people in such a short time!  As a story, though, it's extremely engaging and even thought-provoking.  Could a society really be as corrupt as the one portrayed here?  As cutthroat and ugly?  If you know your history you know the answer is yes, and you can only read on as the characters pursue their individual goals and try to keep their heads above the fast-rising tides of change that are gripping this version of Thailand.  \\"The Windup Girl\\" has reaped much critical praise, deservedly, and hopefully we will see many more works from this young author.  I gave it four stars because I have read plenty of works similar to this one in tone and subject matter (post-Dark Ages), and though this one was quite good, it doesn't break as much new ground as I would have hoped.  Very good but not one of the greats, is my final verdict.  Certainly worth buying.

So I would recommend this book if you would like a nice collection of structures and forms for memory-jogging or getting ideas, but if you are just beginning, I would heed the suggestions of other reviewers and look for a book by Shereen LaPlantz.

B000002AQH|4.0|I owned the 1972 Decca recording by Britten and his Aldeburgh forces for years without feeling much of an urge to take it down from my shelves for two reasons: first, I could not get past my antipathy for either Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau or Peter Pears with their ungrateful tone - one gravelly, the other strangulated - and both pecking away at the text like canaries at a cuttlefish; secondly, the melodic discursiveness of Part One, beginning with a dismally pedestrian overture consisting of a listless, repeated, downward-falling semi-quaver figure followed by some of Schumann's least melodious writing always failed to engage me long enough to persevere.<br /><br />I am in agreement with a previous reviewer who recommends starting your listening with Part 2, which is much closer to Mahler's inspiration in its whole-hearted response to Goethe's mystical, metaphysical vision. This music was of course written a good few years before Part One and as such is much fresher, inventive and devoid of the stolidity and the calcification of Schumann's genius which marked the inexorable encroachment of his mental illness. The second part really is like a different work from the sclerotic, episodic and disjointed Part One.<br /><br />Abbado's direction is too polite to bring out the full dramatic potential of the music but he has the advantages of a superb Berlin Philharmonic and a very starry cast. Terfel, here aged only 28, is in finest voice: rich, noble, vibrant - and one is struck afresh by how clear and idiomatic his German is. One could wish that he sang the other bass parts, too, including Mephistopheles, as he is far more firm and alluring than the rather pedestrian Rootering; Harry Peeters as Pater Profundus is superior to him, too. Endrik Wottrich's neat, flexible, slightly \\"Germanic\\" tenor is far preferable to Pears' bleating. Karita Mattila is large and lovely of tone if not especially moving or vulnerable as Gretchen. The finest female voice is that of gorgeous, fluting soprano Barbara Bonney in various roles; future stars like Susan Graham takes three smaller parts, too.<br /><br />If you want this music, there really is no competition - or indeed another recording - unless you are attached to the other very capable singers for Britten and actively enjoy the sound of those I do not. Apart from the obvious quality of both the recorded sound of this live performance and also of its participants, it is worth acquiring almost solely to hear Bryn Terfel at his youthful best, at his most expressive and communicative and always luscious of voice, from the gentlest of falsetto pianissimo to his gleaming, forte top notes .Bonney in various roles; future stars like Susan Graham takes three smaller parts, too. 0525478817|5.0|I woke up early in the morning with a sinking feeling and tears in my eyes. Had something terrible happened the night before? No, I had only read John Green's \\"The Fault in Our Stars\\".  It's a powerful book. I had read \\"An Abundance of Katherines\\" a couple weeks earlier, and I laughed at one sentence and cried at the next. \\"The Fault in Our Stars\\" went one step further - what started out as a laugh ended as a cry; I really laughed and cried at the same time.   It's no wonder that John's favorite band is the Mountain Goats. (It's my favorite band too, and I probably wouldn't have heard of it if not for John.) Darnielle's three-minute songs and Green's unputdownable one-evening books are in the same incredible boat. John's novels and the Mountain Goat's lyrics share a grasp of the poetry of sadness and simplicity, a minimalism which leaves out everything but raw humanity.   The book is (at least in part) about how to remain human and happy without giving up honesty. On one level, it is a question about what a novel should look like. The theme of the contrast between two types of novel runs through the entire book. There's your typical - cheesy, overly optimistic, making holy that which is just human - cancer novel - a novel, in short,  which compromises its honesty in favor of optimism; and then there's the fictional Peter Van Houten's \\"An Imperial Affliction\\", which ends up erring on the side of pessimism. This device of \\"book within book\\" is a daring one, as there is an implicit claim that John's book manages to steer clear of the pitfalls of both approaches. And steer clear he does - as well as fulfilling the unspoken promise to give the reader an ending rivalling the one in Van Houten's novel.   On another level, the distinction between the two types of novel is of course a distinction between different ways of living our lives in the face of suffering. In the end, the book says no more than to live and love before it is too late - and to not be too afraid of being hurt. And I think this is the best we can do when it comes balancing honesty and humanity. In a new and poignant variation on the \\"'Tis better to have loved and lost...\\" theme, one of the ending lines in the novel is \\"You don't get to choose if you get hurt in this world, old man, but you do have some say in who hurts you.\\"   Having said all this, there is a - perhaps minor, but still disappointing - mistake in the novel. Cantor's discovery that there are different sizes of infinity does /not/ give a solution to Zeno's Paradox. The fact that there can be smaller and larger infinities does not help, because the only infinities involved are the countable infinity of sequences of rational numbers and the uncountable infinity of the continuum - and they are the same for both Achilles and the turtle. And this brings us to the crux of the matter - when Hazel says that \\"of course\\" there is a bigger infinite set of numbers between 0 and 2 than between 0 and 1, she is displaying a complete ignorance of Cantor's work. In fact, one of Cantor's groundbreaking (and clearly extremely unintuitive, judging by Hazel's \\"of course\\") results was that the size of the set of real numbers from 0 to 1 is the same as the size of the set of numbers from 0 to 2 (under a suitable definition of what it means for two sets to have the same size - for the curious, two sets have the same size if and only if one can \\"pair together\\" elements of one set with elements of the other without leaving any out). John could have read all of this in a Wikipedia article - that he didn't, and that neither his editiors nor his more mathematically-minded friends caught the mistake is a severe disappointment. (One could argue that neither Van Houten nor Hazel were mathematicians and so it was okay for them to be wrong. But the uninformed reader will doubtless assume they are correct and take away from the novel a misconception about mathematics and philosophy.)  It is a mark of my high opinion of the book that Igive it five stars despite this glaring mistake. After all, it is not every day that a novel makes you laugh and cry at the exact same moment.

There are loads of battle sequences to keep the action ante up and the BLU RAY picture quality is immaculate throughout (it comes as a 'Double Play' set and there's a '3D' version too). There's even a believable 'attraction' between Jason Momoa and the gorgeous Rachel Nichols as Tamara the 'pure blood'. There's also the ever-watchable Ron Perlman as Corin (Conan's father) and Nonso Anozie plays Conan's loyal sidekick Artus - a man with Byzantine dreadlocks and personal hygiene issues.<br /><br />And yet this new remake grows tiresome very quickly - rather like \\"The Prince Of Persia\\" did. I think the main culprit is the incredibly derivative storyline - we've seen and heard all of this epic warrior and his journey nonsense before - and way too many times too. A boy witnesses his father and entire village be slaughtered - grows up to be a fierce warrior - systematically avenges all involved - meets fab-looking dolly bird en-route - saves the world from the forces of darkness - returns to a nicer mud hut at the end - has a shower (wash the blood and entrails off) - and settles in with a nice cup of cocoa and the Downton Abbey Christmas special. It sounds like great fun - it sounds rip-roaring - and at times it is. But it also feels a little empty-headed somehow. It's hard to get excited about this - and I'd admit to fast forwarding on more than one occasion. I also doubt I'd want to watch it again - so it's a rental rather than a purchase.<br /><br />Still - if you're up for a bit of a He-Man actioner - you could do worse - you could try Poker night at the Scunthorpe Debate Society...ion too). 1451648537|5.0|The number of reviews of this book, on Amazon.com, has already exceeded 900. This is quite staggering and undoubtedly reflects the public interest in Jobs; the person and his achievements. Interestingly though, very few of the 900 reviews make any mention of the book's handling of Jobs battle with cancer. And yet, Isaacson dedicated 3 chapters to this saga. So rather than just repeat what most others have said, that it is a book worth reading, I will comment a little more on the cancer theme.<br /><br />After reading the book one is left with little doubt that the physical and psychological realities of living with cancer had a significant impact on Jobs as a person. Initially he confronts the disease in a typically rebellious way, then he accepts more conventional treatments - but usually on his terms. Finally, we see a more reflective Steve Jobs, accepting the inevitable, but still believing he could have an influence on future developments in the technological world.<br /><br />There has been comment that Jobs chose some approaches to managing his cancer that were not \\"mainstream\\" and that if he had listened to more people, and accepted conventional treatment earlier on, he may have lived longer. But Isaacson threads this viewpoint into the story in such a way as to suggest that if Jobs had taken a conventional approach he wouldn't have been \\"Steve Jobs\\".<br /><br />Finally, I think that the book shows that Jobs, in his own way demonstrated that regardless of the steady progression of a life threatening cancer, by being \\"true to yourself\\" it is possible to stay in control of the situation - at least in a psychological sense - and to keep living a good life for as long as that is possible.

B0068RHGJM|2.0|I guess the first thing that needs to be cleared up in any Nightwish album review, is that I am not a \\"Tarja Troll\\" and actually rank \\"Dark Passion Play\\" as my 2nd favorite Nightwish album behind only \\"Once\\". Actually, Anette isn't even the problem I have with this album - her performance is the BEST by far. The album quite simply doesn't \\"rock\\" as much as DPP. (Dark Passion Play) I think Tuomas simply pushed this circus them for the album a bit too far, and I found it interwoven into some of the songs unnecessarily, or interrupting in the middle and killing the momentum of a song entirely. Dark Passion Play is far superior, so I again reiterate that this is not an \\"Anette isn't good in Nightwish\\" because that album was fantastic. I'll briefly break down the tracks, since I imagine most people won't like my review because I didn't blindly \\"Oh masterpiece, five stars, bravo!\\" like many people are doing. This album feels like solo material from each band member that was piece together. A few tracks have like 3 or 4 musical themes interwoven together, and I just don't think it flows well at all. If you want circus music, cut a track or two with circus music - don't put it in half the songs.   #1 - Taikatalvi: Meh, not really a song IMO. It's just random talking and instrumentals. It's fine I guess.   #2 - Storytime: The first full song. The first time I heard this I wasn't overly impressed. It's not a bad song (one of the better ones) but I wanted a killer song to open the album like DPP had \\"The Poet and the Pendulum (arguably the first or second most epic song the band has ever done behind maybe Ghost Love Score). Maybe I expect too much from this song opening the album. The song certainly has the Nightwish feel, that's indisputable.   #3 - Ghost River: Quite possibly the worst Nightwish song ever. I don't like the feel of the song, and Marco isn't really singing, he's chanting stuff in an irritating octave. Marco's voice isn't shot, I just really don't like how he'ssinging at all on this song (and I like him in past efforts, so it's not a knock on him). There's too much going on in this song, it has horrible flow.   #4 - Slow, Love, Slow: Picture Anette going back in time, to the 1940's Gangsta lifestyle in America. Now picture her sitting on a piano singing to a room full of people. That's basically what this song is, and if I am not mistaken, Tuomas said he was inspired from something from that era. People will either like this song or not like it IMO. I'll probably skip it more than I listen to it.   #5 - I Want My Tears Back: After back to back not very good songs, the band gets back on track with a better song. I've seen a few reviewers really geeking out over this song, not sure why. It's decent, but pretty much EVERY song on Dark Passion Play is better than this. The last third of the song turns into a cross between the Islander, and some random circus music.   #6 - Scaretale: I like how the song begins. I think this song had \\"opening track\\" written all over it. Has a slight \\"Poet and the Pendulum\\" feel to the beat. Then around the 4 min mark, a full on circus them emerges in the dead middle of the song, and kills any and all momentum this song had. Massive, massive letdown. Not a fan at all. If I wanted to hear circus music, I could go buy \\"Best of Barnum and Bailey vol 1\\" but I didn't. The rest of the song pisses me off.   # 7 - Arabesque: Arabian music. No vocals. Give it a listen, see what you think. It's 2:36, so it's almost over before you can form an opinion. It's ok I guess, no real strong feelings either way.   #8 - Turn Loose The Mermaids: Not bad. It has a slow feel with the bulk of the tracks effectiveness being carried by Anette's voice. I like it.  #9 - Rest Calm: The beginning drags a bit, feels like a complete filler track. I like Anette's singing, but the musical direction on this track feels highly uninspired like they just wanted to finish the song. Not crazy about this song at all, the flow is all over the place, with a random chorus of kids joining the band in the middle of the song.   #10 - The Crow, The Owl And The Dove: Doesn't even feel like a Nightwish song. Actually, that's the problem with this album - a lot of it feels like random solo material from the band mushed together. That's the problem.   #11 - Last Ride Of The Day: The heaviest track since....the opening track? Good song, it's a shame more song with a slightly heavier theme were not included on this album.   #12 - Song of Myself: Starts off well enough, slowly builds into something that feels like it will be an explosion of rock/metal worth headbanging too. Fast guitars, solid flow and drumming - check. The song is also 13 min long and seems to have a few different themes to the music, but at least every theme is good. Then the 7:30 spot or so, we go into some random talking and we once again kill the momentum of the song. I feel like I'm listening to an audio book, not a song. Eventually the music disappears and various band members are just talking. Boring.   #13 - Imaginerium: Not really a song, just pieces of music from other songs.   I'm not very excited for this album, and I can tell I do not need to \\"let it grow on me\\" I know what I like and do not like pretty quickly. Dark Passion Play was great, but this album just doesn't do it  for me at all.   I sincerely hope many others like this album for one huge reason: I do not want to see album sales be poor and Tumoas start to think \\"Oh maybe Anette is the problem, maybe I need a new singer\\" because the problem is NOT her. At all. It's the rest of the band this time around. Tumoas should of focused on writing good songs, not adhering to a circus theme and letting it muddle his normally exceptional song writing ability. I still plan to see them on tour and will likely enjoy them immensely. Just not a fan of this album. Sorry.
